B000SOZXJG|5.0|Whale Rider was a wonderfully directed movie with a powerful message.<br /><br /> The movie centered on a teenage girl, Paikea, whose mother and twin brother died during the childbirth/delivery. Paikea's father obviously devastated at the loss of his wife and son- was interrupted by the arrival of his father and mother, the father obviously not caring about the sensitivity of the situation instead he insisted upon seeing the boy.  The grandfather, Koro, after learning of the boy's death shunned his granddaughter and insisted on chanting over the poor little angel who had passed away.  Koro had believed that his grandson would be the heir/chief for their tribe who would bring about change as we found out later in the story, once the boy died the grandfather was distraught over who would lead his people. Paikea in the mean time grew up living with her grandparents because her father had taken off not long after the death of his wife and became an artist.  He traveled extensively throughout Europe and only visited occasionally- mainly because of the strained relationship you saw between the father and son.<br /> Koro eventually grew to care for Paikea but insisted on embracing the old ways that the next chief should be a man as with many traditions he was rock solid in his beliefs. Koro eventually set up a school to teach the first born young men the traditions and customs to hopefully choose a new chief among the families of the tribe.  Paikea who only wanted the approval and love of her grandfather sought out to learn these traditions unfortunately Koro was disgusted by this and sought to dissuade Paikea every chance he got.  Paikea eventually learned each part and excelled at every turn with the help of her uncle, Rawiri.<br /> Towards the later part of the movie, we learn that whales which were a spiritual symbol of the Maori tribe as it is believed that their ancestors rode on the backs of whales when they entered our world so whales were a revered symbol for thetribe.  A few beached whales were discovered by Koro who insisted that someone were to blame for this and wouldn't let Paikea help in the efforts to get the biggest one back out to sea.  Paikea ended up riding the whale for some time and was later found unconscious, she recovered and the entire tribe celebrated the announcement of their new chief, Paikea even her grandfather looked proud as they rowed into the ocean together as a tribe.<br /> Whale Rider demonstrates how in many older religious beliefs that were male dominated, fought and resisted women being allowed to serve in seats of power.  Through this story it is demonstrated how a teenage girl who strives to be this chief/leader of her tribe, mainly to prove her significance to her grandfather.  Also shown is how as generations evolve and change certain ideals among indigenous religion view such as in this story when Koro establishes that the heir must be male- as it was told in ancient oral histories and through traditions.  The progression of the views and beliefs are a common side effect to mainstream modern times to influence some indigenous views that can be interrupted in a fluid manner.

B004J4WKTW|3.0|The Night Circus is, simply put, a collection of images, visuals & sensory pleasures brought to life by words in the most fantastical of worlds.  Told in a confusing jumble of back and forth time hops, Morgenstern tells the story of a most tragic competition. Marco & Celia are raised sternly, without love and for one reason alone - to win a competition.  To that end Marco and Celia train relentlessly for years practicing magic, illusions, spells, and all sorts of esoteric & mystical arts. Little is know about the competition. When will it take place? Who will be the competitors? How does one win?<br /><br />One thing is certain  - the venue. A magical circus that arrives like the night and leaves with the dawn. The circus travels around the world without advertising or fanfare. Meticulously decorated in white, black & silver, the color scheme permeates every aspect of the tents and their acts. And what acts! Ice gardens, wishing trees aflame in candles, cloud rooms, gravity defying acrobats and mystifying illusionists.<br /><br />It isn't long after Marco and Celia join the circus that the participants and venue are identified.  Celia and Marco have been set up to compete against each other in the most bizarre of competitions with the most tragic of outcomes. They must compete against each other, creating grander spectacles and illusions while keeping the circus running because the circus has become an extension of their own spirits and must feed off of them to survive. The winner is the one who can handle the strain the longest and there can only be one. Given these circumstances will Celia and Marco ever  have a chance to live outside the competition and embrace their love?<br /><br />Morgenstern's novel is slow and plodding. The words carefully chosen but not displaying any particular flair. Rather, it is the imagery that rules the book. Wondrous  imaginative creations described in meticulous details. Morgenstern's imagination is like that of a painter and the novel is never more alive than when an outfit or magical piece is described.<br /><br />Action is slow in this novel and the romance a retrained passion expressed by shared glances and brought to life in the amazing acts each develops in an effort to give life to emotions that can't be uttered.  But The Night Circus is without a doubt a tragic love story wrapped in greed, vanity and mania. Many may disagree but this is certainly not an HEA in the traditional sense. Instead it is a cerebral tale of man's quest for control and dominance of this world around him. An obsession - if you will - with being right at whatever cost.<br /><br />The book left me with some incredible images but over all made me feel terribly sad and despondent.vel is never more alive than when an outfit or magical piece is described.<br /><br />Action is slow in this novel and the romance a retrained passion expressed by shared glances and brought to life in the amazing acts each develops in an effort to give life to emotions that can't be uttered.  But The Night Circus is without a doubt a tragic love story wrapped in greed, vanity and mania. Many may disagree but this is certainly not an HEA in the traditional sense. Instead it is a cerebral tale of man's quest for control and dominance of this world around him. An obsession - if you will - with being right at whatever cost.<br /><br />The book left me with some incredible images but over all made me feel terribly sad and despondent.

B003MC5N28|5.0|The Giver is a relatively short book, at least now that I'm adult.  When I was in middle school, the two hundred-odd pages probably would have given me a little more reason to gulp.  It wasn't assigned reading at the school I went to (probably because it wasn't published until the year I graduated high school), and despite its classification as science fiction, despite the fact (or maybe because of it) that it was a Newbery Medal winner, the picture of an old man and trees on the front cover were sufficient to keep me from ever picking it up in the past.<br /><br />It's really not science fiction.  It takes place in the future, but so did 1984, Fahrenheit 451, and A Clockwork Orange relative to when they were written.  We need a subgenre for dystopian fiction, not because I demand you get your peanut butter out of my chocolate, but because there's quite a lot of it and it really deserves attention as its own thing (as you can tell from the titles dropped in the previous sentence.  The Giver is a story set in a world in which a citizen government has completely ordered every aspect of daily life.  Children don't have birthdays; they all graduate to a new age together, abandoning the elements of their childhood their age group has outgrown and taking on new responsibilities as a class.  Adults are coupled together by a selection committee, and 1-year old children raised in a nurturing hospital are given to them to raise.  Instincts toward joy and love are tamped down, and negative feelings carefully protected against.  Even concepts as simple as colors and uphill have been weeded out.  The story follows a child whose assigned career path is to experience everything the society has left behind - pleasure and pain - in order to advise the society when dealing with things their atrophied emotional life leaves them unprepared for.<br /><br />What's Good: I thought the story was surprisingly sophisticated.  It's been pointed out to me (and perhaps rightly so) that to a middle-schooler the story would perhaps be more blatantly black-and-white, that the moral choices would be clear to the reader and this dystopian society would, in fact, be obviously dystopian. But the author was clearly an adult, and I believe this is the kind of book one would expect to stick in a young reader's head and mature with them. I think that unlike the above-mentioned dystopias, it's not actually clear that the world is dystopic. People are happy. Yes, there is no \\"love\\".  There isn't joy, and there isn't art. Some ugly little truths (like death) are swept under the rug.  But the tradeoff against depression, against heartache, poverty and famine, a life of fear, a life of continual disillusionment is deliberate, and (at least at some point) consciously made, and successfully made.  The way the society is presented, there really is no underbelly seedier than whatever they abandoned in the past.  There are no clear answers, and that's unusual for a dystopian novel, and especially unusual for young adult.<br /><br />What's Bad: Off-hand, not much.  There are some questions about how some things in the book would actually work (like memory transfer through touch), but they're conceits, not just poor world-building.  The characters are rich and varied, despite the constraints their society puts on them, and the book isn't long enough for this \\"dystopia of boring\\" to drag.  I can't say I actually enjoyed the book, but it -is- dystopian, after all.<br /><br />The Takeaway: I recommend it!  Even if I didn't really like it, it's good!  It's not spoon-feeding the reader a self-soothing opinion; it actually promotes thought. (Plus, it's short, and usually cheap because it's on school reading lists, so you don't have too much of an excuse.) B002U3CCBK|4.0|Don't expect a repeat of the movie that was based on this book of the same title.  Although the opening chapters and all the way through to the next to last chapters are similar, the ending is quite different.  This book is a pleasant and easy read.  The author, Paul Torday, has penned a novel whose plot is making it possible for salmon to inhabit and spawn in the Yemen River in the eastern, desert-like nation country of Yemen.  Of course this is just about in the realm of the impossible but for a Yemeni Sheik who has the faith (and the funds) to make it happen and a respected fisheries scientist who can realize the Sheik's dream. B008LZHA3G|4.0|...and a hell of a storm is upon us.  \\"It's gonna sweep my world away,\\" but it gets worse -- \\"It's gonna blow the sky apart.\\"  While \\"Duquesne Whistle\\" is a cheerful, toe-tapping number, the lyrics are anything but carefree.  TEMPEST is an apocalyptic vision, which is quite appropriate given A) that the world is supposed to end in December 2012 according to a widespread misinterpretation of the Mayan calendar, and B) the reality of catastrophic climate change, which we are headed toward like a runaway train.<br /><br />Dylan is no longer the blue-eyed son, \\"goin' out 'fore the rain starts a-fallin'.\\"  He is now the grizzled old man, making his way through the storm, which is growing stronger by the day.  He can now sing \\"I was so much younger then, I'm older than that now.\\"  Yes, he gave up his role as protest singer and prophet long ago.  His latter-day persona has been the roving gambler with pencil-thin mustache, the worldly rogue, a God-fearing man fully immersed in the profane world and its pleasures.<br /><br />But the prophet still speaks, and it is perhaps the more prominent place of that submerged voice that makes TEMPEST more compelling to me than his acclaimed recent Trilogy.  In \\"Narrow Way,\\" a great propulsive shuffle, Dylan mentions \\"the bleeding wound in the heart of town.\\"  Is he talking to God when he says \\"If I can't work up to you, you'll surely have to work down to me someday\\"?  The song is full of \\"Isis\\"-style bravado, but a friend tells him \\"Be gentle brother, be gentle and pray.\\"<br /><br />The dark heart of the album runs from \\"Pay In Blood\\" through \\"Scarlet Town\\" and \\"Early Roman Kings\\" to \\"Tin Angel.\\"  In these songs, Dylan clearly dons the mask of evil.  \\"Pay In Blood\\" is another pleasant-sounding tune, featuring steel guitar, but Dylan sings in the voice of the Devil, rasping and choking.  \\"I pay in blood, but not my own.\\"  \\"The more I die, the more I live.\\"  Could this be the Wall Street bankers getting bailed out?  The State sending young troops to die?  \\"Scarlet Town\\" features banjo and fiddle, as well as a nice guitar solo, one of the few on the album.  When Dylan sings \\"all things are beautiful in their time\\" in this very dark setting, the listener can't help but sense that everything is broken and degraded, that actually *nothing* is beautiful.  These songs suggest to me that Dylan may have been reading Cormac McCarthy, in particular [[ASIN:B004UM9UAA Blood Meridian]], his epic novel of American violence.  \\"Early Roman Kings\\" is a slow, relaxed, loping, and altogether excellent rendition of the well-known riff used by Muddy Waters for \\"Mannish Boy.\\"  In it \\"peddlers and meddlers destroyed your city, and they'll destroy you too.\\"  Could this refer to Halliburton?  When Dylan sings \\"I could strip you of life, strip you of breath,\\" he sounds to me like McCarthy's character The Judge from \\"Blood Meridian.\\"<br /><br />I don't find the last three songs to be especially compelling.  \\"Tin Angel,\\" which features banjo, is a story of a love triangle which ends badly.  The main character, known only as \\"The Boss,\\" is another despicable character, one who orders the use of \\"the cheapest labor money can buy.\\"  The epic \\"Tempest\\" is the story of the sinking of the Titanic, complete with Leonardo DiCaprio.  If Dylan is not using the Titanic as a metaphor for society steaming toward ecological doom then he is more oblivious to the world around him than I consider to be very likely.  His persona inhabits the past, a timeless past of Americana, but the songs of evil on TEMPEST are clearly informed by \\"modern times.\\"  Musically the song has little variation, but there are three instrumental breaks.  The first two feature fiddle, and the final reprise without fiddle after God's hand has rendered judgement on \\"good, bad, rich, and poor\\" is poignant for its absence.<br /><br />Dylan's road-tested band sounds great, with Tony Garnier on bass, George G. Receli on drums, Donnie Herron on steel guitar, banjo, violin &mandolin, Charlie Sexton and Stu Kimball on guitar, and David Hidalgo sitting in on guitar, accordion, and violin.  The music they play is simple and solid, blending blues and rock with a little country flavoring.<br /><br />*** *** ***<br /><br />I have been one of the few apparently impervious to Dylan's charms since his comeback beginning in 1997.  I don't have anything bad to say about the latter-day Trilogy (Time Out of Mind, Love & Theft, Modern Times), they just haven't spoken to me.  I can't really say why I find TEMPEST more compelling when it's the same band and sounds so similar.<br /><br />The best I can offer is that the dark, apocalyptic mood rings my bell.<br /><br />(REVIEW #90 -- 9/17/12)mandolin, Charlie Sexton and Stu Kimball on guitar, and David Hidalgo sitting in on guitar, accordion, and violin. Playing this game makes me think that Anuman certainly knows how to make games (and boy do they crank out lots of cookie-cutter games just like this one), I don't think they understand why some hidden object games are so much fun.  Apparently, it takes more than just, \\"Hey, there's money in hidden object games, let's make hidden object games!\\"<br /><br />Although I have purchased a few of these games from Anuman (and dozens from other companies), I have yet to find one I would consider very good.  [[ASIN:B007W24NT8 XIII - Lost Identity]] is probably their best one.  Really, you'd probably be better off spending the same money on just about any hidden object title from one of the other major HO developers (G5, Big Fish, Alawar, etc.) and get a better game.<br /><br />The hidden object scenes are almost always far too easy, and the mini-games are seem pretty pointless (tap on an image when it appears, solve a 6-piece jigsaw puzzle, make 20 matches in a very basic, untimed match-3 game).  You really can't ever lose any of them.<br /><br />The Puzzles are mindless. You can almost always clearly see the solution, you just have to spend the time going through the steps to solve them. The ones that have the most potential are mechanically very buggy -- the sliding puzzles take more time struggling to actually move a piece in the direction you want them to go then figuring out where to slide them.  Nothing tops the frustration of swiping left to right on a playing card in the hedge maze 20-30 times because it refuses to slide anywhere but up and down.  I literally had Alice 2 spaces from the exit of the puzzle with nothing blocking her, but I could not get her to move to the right.<br /><br />Although they look like a lot of fun from the screenshots, I would steer clear of Anuman hidden object games in general, but definitely don'twaste your time or money on Alice in Wonderland.<br />-------<br />If you found this review helped you learn more about the game without having to buy it yourself, please hit \\"Yes\\".

B00007E8O5|5.0|After Boggy Depot, Alice in Chains briefly got back together to record a couple songs and release a compilation set, but once again fell apart right after: so Jerry stepped back up to the plate and made it on his own. This double album is the way Jerry wished it to be released from the start: the earlier single disc set was a compromise with the record company nervous about a project of this size. Now that we have the full package, Degradation Trip vol. 1&2 is different than his Debut, but is no less strong.<br /><br />Other reviews do the `song-by-song' approach so I'll just give my thoughts on the set and point out some of the standouts in my opinion. This set is less experimental than Boggy Depot, and focuses more on Cantrell's signature heavy, sludgy riffing balanced with some gorgeous acoustic numbers. The album is quite a bit heavier than Boggy Depot, with songs like \\"Spiderbite\\", \\"Hellbound\\", \\"Anger Rising\\" and \\"She Was My Girl\\" rivaling Soundgarden's Batmotorfinger in terms of raw, heavy, in your face metal. Though in true Cantrell style we get gorgeous acoustic songs like \\"Gone\\", \\"31/32\\" and \\"Give it a Name\\" to balance the experience, and songs like \\"Siddhartha\\", \\"Feel the Void\\" and \\"What it Takes\\" recall the creepy, skin crawling style of AiC hits like \\"Died\\" and \\"Get Born Again\\".<br /><br />Jerry mostly sings at a much lower pitch than he did on his previous solo effort, matching the darker, dirtier sound of the album, though he rises up on the acoustic numbers to keep those songs refreshing breaks from all the darkness. As far as other things, I do feel the greater prominence of the Bass over Boggy Depot is important since many of the songs are slow and sludgy, and nothing is as dank and sludgy as a well-played bass line.<br /><br />Overall Cantrell's second solo effort is a different sort of flavor from his debut, but it's still amazing and shows off how strong he is on his own. I honestly feel than Jerry Cantrell is just as strong on his own as he is with Alice in Chains, and I wish his solo efforts got greater recognition.

0452289963|3.0|A New Earth: Awakening to your life's purpose by Eckhart Tolle, Michael Joseph (Penguin), 2005, 330 ff.<br /><br />Tolle's earlier book, The Power of Now, was an introspective on how to mould the self into a spiritual being by abandoning the ego. This book, which takes its title from a biblical quotation, presents essentially the same material but is directed towards helping others achieve the same goal, primarily by example. His aim, he says, is to encourage others to a `transformation of consciousness' by abandoning the idea of ego and self.<br /><br />His second chapter on `Ego: The Current State of Humanity' is indeed an accurate reflection of the materialist individualism that has been prevalent in western society for the past few decades. Tolle is right when he says that too many people derive their sense of identity from material things; but he prefers Sartre's view of identity to the Cartesian \\"cogito\\", where identity and consciousness are bound up with thinking - Tolle has a problem with our being able to think, for thinking just happens and is not a voluntary action.<br /><br />The next two chapters continue this exploration of the nature of the ego until we move back to the \\"pain-body\\" that Tolle introduced in the earlier book. Like Hegel and Marx, he recognizes the destructive role of alienation, of otherness, which prevents us from feeling a sense of identity with other people and our environment. This sense of feeling hurt by others is what Tolle describes as the \\"pain-body\\" - `an accumulation of old emotional pain.' The aim in life is to overcome the negative emotions of the pain-body through \\"satori\\", a Zen term for the `arising of inner spaciousness', and there is a whole chapter on `The Discovery of Inner Space'.<br /><br />The book ends with a chapter on The New Earth as Tolle sees it. The theme of the book is one that has been explored by many authors, for example, Gill Edwards (Living Magically), Louise Hay (You Can Heal Your Life; unlike Tolle, Louise believes we can choose our thoughts), Philip Jacobs (One Self - note! No sense here that we have to abandon the idea of `self' for enlightenment) and, most eminently, The Dalai Lama (The Four Noble Truths). So, while the message here of 'living in the present' is laudable, the 300 pages seem almost interminable and The Power of Now, and several other authors, say essentially the same things much more succinctly.<br /><br />[[ASIN:1577314808 The Power of Now: A Guide to Spiritual Enlightenment]]<br />[[ASIN:0722535503 The Four Noble Truths]]Louise believes we can choose our thoughts), Philip Jacobs (One Self - note! B008RV5K5E|3.0|Let me preface this review by saying that I read the original graphic novel series many years ago. I understand that such a work as done by Frank Miller could be difficult to translate into an animated film simply because of the nature in which it was written and the way in which the artwork was constructed. All in all, while I did feel some disappointment with this film, I did find it to be an entertaining re-telling of Frank Miller's original work. It seemed to hold the proper tone that Miller was trying to capture in that an aging Batman has to contend with a society that no longer wants his presence as well as his age handicapping his abilities.<br /><br />One thing that did bother me was the lack of \\"voice-over\\" narration by Batman as seen in the graphic novel. While the lack of this narration did not detract from the film and it's impact, I think there would have been an even stronger impact upon viewers if we could hear those intimate, calculating, emotional thoughts that Batman had during each encounter. A perfect example is when he first comes back onto the scene and is climbing a rope to reach the bad guys. One bad guy says that Batman has to be old by now. In response to this, the Batman voice-over says, \\"Old enough to need to use my legs to climb a rope\\". This occurs in the comics, not the film, but I think it really allows the audience a peek into the mindframe and mindset of Batman while he engages in his crime-fighting activities. As I said, it is not a mood-killer that it was left out of the film, but I do think the creative team behind the film kind of missed out on a very good opportunity to really suck the audience of the film into the mind of Batman.<br /><br />Another thing that bothered me was the voice casting for Batman/Bruce Wayne. Yes, Peter Weller did a fine job with this, and I give him his due credit for giving life to an aging Batman. I also understand why the creative team might have wanted to not utilize the legendary Kevin Conroy (the one and only true voice for Batman from the animated series of the 90's, the Justice League animated series, the animated movie between Batman Begins and The Dark Knight, and the Arkham video games) for the voice in an effort to open up people's minds to the concept of more than one person being able to bring Batman to life. Unfortunately, I feel like the creative team missed the boat a bit on this one. For anyone who ever watched the animated series from the 90's, you may recall that they did a storytelling episode that actually made use of Frank Miller's graphic novel. In specific, they showed a scene that blended Batman's initial attack against the Mutants with his final fight against the Mutant's leader in which he utter's the infamous expression, \\"This isn't a trash heap, it's an operating table, and I'm the suregon.\\" In this instance, the creative team behind the animated series made, in my opinion, an absolutely genius decision to utilize Michael Ironside (\\"Jester\\" from the film Top Gun) as the voice of Batman. I really and truly wish the team behind the Dark Knight Returns movie had decided to retain his voice or, if they had indeed tried to go that route, that Mr. Ironside had accepted any offer to do said voice for this film. While Peter Weller certainly exemplified the cold and dark visage of Batman, Michael Ironside, in just a ten minute clip, truly seemed to epitomize the dark, gritty, dangerousness of a Batman who, while having grown older, has also become a lot more calculating and deadly with his experiences. It was a truly missed opportunity.<br /><br />Overall, I was generally happy with the film and look forward to seeing how they put the rest of Miller's story into film in part 2, particularly the verbal back and forth and eventual combat between Batman and Superman.

Born Ulrich Tolle in Germany he changed his name from his admiration for the writings of the German mystic, Meister Eckhart. Self taught from the age of 13 he nevertheless graduated from both the University of London and Cambridge University. This book is essentially an approachable and non-religious distillation of the core teachings of Buddhism.<br /><br />The book is written as a dialogue between the author and an imaginary sceptical inquisitor. The quest is a search for release from the pain-body as Eckhart calls it, which has elements of Jung's shadow about it but is probably closest to the Buddhist notion of \\"dukkha\\" - unease, discontent or disquiet, the continual search for something more and better. Eckhart is encouraging us to `leave mind and find consciousness', to set aside `the delusion of time' for `nothing exists outside the now'. Or, as a wise mystic told me, `The past is gone - it cannot be changed or rewritten. The future is only a series of wishes and dreams - or fears, of consequences of past misdeeds. The present is the only place to be.'  To quote Eckhart again: `The power of Now is your consciousness liberated from thought forms'. It is about `Finding your invisible and indestructible reality' in order to slow down the aging process, strengthen the immune system and encourage enlightened relationships. The setting aside of the ego is something many spiritual disciplines encourage.<br /><br />Whether you are already on a spiritual quest or are seeking the key to open one, this is a worthwhile read. My only serious criticism would be that it is rather repetitive. The essential message could have been presented in just a few pages: but then, a message that is so alien to the kind of life that most of us lead needs to be repeated in many different ways.<br /><br />[[ASIN:0722535503 The Four Noble Truths]]ut then, a message that is so alien to the kind of life that most of us lead needs to be repeated in many different ways.<br /><br />[[ASIN:0722535503 The Four Noble Truths]]

0143119680|4.0|This is a huge novel! According to amazon.com it's 592 pages but it seems a bit longer. This is the first of a trilogy of books and the debut novel of author Deborah Harkness.<br />I had heard a lot about this book while I was at the 2012 Comic Con. It was toted as an adult version of Twilight meets Harry Potter (interesting, yes?) but before I embarked on it, I wanted to read some reviews and boy do they vary. Usually when I read reviews, you can get a good idea usually that people either love it or hate it. Sometimes 9 times out of 10 people will love a book (street team aside) and you'll have a few that will hate it for one reason or another. The flip side can be people hate it except for a few. Anywho, If I'm unsure about a book, I do look at the reviews to get a good idea what I'm in for. This book however, is one of the first that I've run into where you can't make heads or tails of it from the reviews. People either absolutely love it or the absolutely loathe it for again, one reason or another and it seems to be split right down the middle. So to add to all the confusion, here's my review:<br />I WAS ABSOLUTELY BORED OUT OF MY MIND......In the beginning. Now I never try to give you spoilers or take you step by step through a book because I don't want to spoil things for you. I'll give you the blurb and the link but usually that's it, then I give my review. In this case, I'll walk you through a bit because the book is so long and I can't possibly not explain parts of it. With that being said, the books starts out with Diana (main character) coming across a book. She then reads parts of the book then puts it back. Oh and by the way,she's a witch with untold power but she refuses to acknowledge her power or use it in any way. She tries to fit in among us lowly humans and becomes a scholar. She's doing research and comes across the enchanted book. Supposedly the book releases a large amount of power upon being found. The power draws other \\"creatures\\" to Diana and the book even though she's put the book back and now it's been lost once again, shocking! One of the main creatures to find her is a vampire named Matthew who becomes the other main character. Diana and Matthew eventually fall in love but you're never quiet sure what Matthew is up to. He's a very complicated character but you almost get the feeling that he's almost too complicated. He has tons of secrets (who wouldn't after thousands of years). When we meet him in the beginning we know he's after the book and also a fellow scholar but as the book goes on, we find out that's only the surface.<br />The first book \\"Discovery Of Witches\\" is basically romance and world building for the trilogy. It's showing the relationship between Diana and Matthew as well as what the following two books in the trilogy will entail. Unfortunately the author seems to think that you need to follow the characters through every single movement of the day. By the time you finish the book, you feel like you were with the characters every step of the day. And the whole book really takes place only over a few weeks. Now with that being said, if you realize what the author is doing and let yourself be totally immersed in this world, it can be quite interesting but if you have no patience for this type of writing and want the whole story wrapped up as quickly as possible, this is not the book for you. I, like you, like a quick tale but this is not it. The author wants you sucked into the world, to identify with either of the two main characters and probably become one. She shares feelings, textures, scents, sounds, emotions, etc. When I say the author goes over every detail, I am not kidding. The story really probably could have been told in 200+ pages with a good editor but it's close to 600 pages and most of that is fill or fluff. Oh and as a word to the wise or to my romance fans, there is no sex scenes in this book. Matthew is totally \\"old fashion\\" in every sense of the word. Even after theydeclare their love for each other and Diana basically throws herself at him, he turns her down to where you think he might even be gay. With that being said, you do however eventually come to see why he's so scared about that level of intimacy. What this book boils down to is almost a classic forbidden romance because of race, yep you heard me, race. In Harkness's world, vampires, witches, demons and humans are the four races that inhabit the world and there is no way they should ever, ever mix. They totally loathe each other and the latter of the 4 do everything in their power to keep the lowly humans from finding out the others exist which includes not mingling with each other and never ever do they have relations but Diana and Matthew will not deny their love. So you can see where this is going......Diana and Matthew do the classic Romeo and Juliet thing and defy all others with a forbidden love. This book takes on politics, romance, paranormal, history, race relations, family and so on. It is so far a classic rebellion story....Viva La France!......oh whoops wrong story.<br />So what's my verdict on this book, well.....if you like forbidden romance, history, immersing yourself in a world totally, world building and are prepared to be in it for the long haul, I think you'll like the books. Remember, the first half of this book is supper slow and pretty much you're in Diana's head although you do meet Matthew early on. Just remember a few key things while reading it, the book (Ashmole) is important. So her early encounter of it, keep in the back of your mind. Characters are important but there's so many it's hard to keep them all straight but as you meet them, remember what you can. And the history and lore are important, so as they spout it out, keep it in the back of your mind as well. Other then that, have fun with it and remember you must have patience for these books.<br />Oh and just as an FYI - There is a movie in the works for this trilogy. Here's the blurb from the authors website: Warner Brothers Pictures has secured the film rights to the All Souls Trilogy. Work is currently underway to adapt the first book, A Discovery of Witches, for the screen. Denise DiNovi and Alison Greenspan are producing the film. Playwright David Auburn, who has received both a Pulitzer Prize and a Tony Award, has been hired to write the screenplay.

0375507256|1.0|I don't much go in for literary novels. However, every once in a while I will read one (Michael Chabon's 'The Amazing Adventures of Kavalier and Klay' or China Mieville's 'Perdido Street Station', for example) that makes me think I might be missing something.<br /><br />This is a highly celebrated novel as both a literary novel (nominated for the Man Booker Prize) and science fiction novel (nominated for the Clarke and Nebula Awards).<br /><br />I will just come right out and say it: I don't get it.<br /><br />Let me qualify that statement some. I get that the book has a complex style. There are six very loosely-related novellas, told in overlapping sections. But in order for this complexity to have a greater meaning or depth, the novellas must have something more in common. Unless I missed something, the only relation between the various protagonists is that they all have a comet-shaped birthmark between their shoulder and their collarbone. That's it. As Dave Barry says, I am not making this up. I wish I could find a deeper thread to the narratives and then I wouldn't feel like I had wasted the last five or six days reading it, but I can't. So I did.<br /><br />Mitchell is successful at giving each protagonist a distinct voice, but he resorts to some cheap tricks to do so. In the novella of Somni~451 he takes every word that begins with 'ex' and truncates that to just 'x'. Pretty weak stuff for a Man Booker Prize nominee. In Zachry's story he uses a un-educated patois reminiscent of Anthony Burgess' 'A Clockwork Orange'. Yes, it gives the novella's narrator a unique voice. The problem is that, like all the other narrators in 'Cloud Atlas', he doesn't really have anything interesting to say.<br /><br />First off you have a young American attorney, Adam Ewing, crossing the Pacific from Australia who rescues an escaped slave. This story is told by means of a diary Ewing keeps which is read by... Robert Frobisher, a cad, conman and composer who is trying to steal music and heirlooms from a syphillitic dying composer (not to mention the composer's wife and daughter) in 1930s Belgium. This novella is told through a series of letters to one Rufus Sixtsmith. Sixtsmith becomes a whistle-blower to... Luisa Rey an aspiring investigative reporter who exposes an unsafe nuclear reactor in late 1970s California. Next we hear the story ('Ordeal', rather) of Timothy Cavendish, a British publisher in contemporary times. Then we jump to Somni~451, a condemned clone-slave (fabricant) who achieves a higher consciousness and becomes a catalyst for a revolution. Finally we hear a story of Zachry, a tribesman in post-Apocalyptic Hawaii who is visited by Meronym a member of the last technologically advanced civilization.<br /><br />The stories are told thusly:<br /><br />1a Adam Ewing<br />2a Roberth Frobisher<br />3a Luisa Rey<br />4a Timothy Cavendish<br />5a Somn_451<br />6 Zachry<br />5b Somni~451<br />4b Timothy Cavendish<br />3b Luisa Rey<br />2b Robert Frobisher<br />1b Adam Ewing<br /><br />Throughout each novella are links to other novellas (with the exception of Zachry's story which is self-contained and is not interrupted). The problem is these are meaningless flourishes. Apparently at some point Timothy Cavendish's story (along with Zachry's tale almost completely pointless) is made into a film which Somni~451 watches the first half of. Great. So what? I am sure when Mitchell outlined the structure of the - an aside: it's not really a novel; it's a collection of novellas - book that must have really looked great to the editor and publisher but without substantive links, it's a gimmick nothing more.<br /><br />I will say that of the six novellas, I enjoyed Somni~451's the most. Somni is a slave working in a future Korean fast-food restaurant. Her existence revolves utterly around the franchise for which she works (Papa Song's) and serving her customers. Fabricants (as they are known) are not supposed to have aspirations and are supposed tobe barely capable of higher thought. However, she is unwittingly subjected to an experiment that causes her ascension (development of a higher consciousness) and she is then removed from the fast-food franchise and thrust into the middle of a burgeoning civil war.<br /><br />Now, I say interesting but that is only in relation to the other novellas. In the Somni~451 novella we are shown a world of cruel corporatism and soul-less consumerism run amok. In fact, in the future, monetary transactions are conducted via the Soul, a subcutaneous chip implanted in purebloods ('real' people as opposed to fabricants).<br /><br />In Somni's future, brand names have been co-opted as nouns. Similar to aspirin now being a common noun and not a brand, in the future a gun is a colt, a computer is a sony and a cigarette is a marlboro. As social criticism it's about as subtle and deep as an Occupy Schenectady rally. And like the entire Occupy movement (and I use that term loosely) it's long on criticism but very, very, very short on solutions.<br /><br />In fact, Mitchell himself as much as admits he doesn't have one because the next novella in the sequence, Zachry's story, takes place in a post-apocalyptic setting.<br /><br />I picked up this book because I had heard from a friend that this movie got rave reviews at the Toronto International Film Festival. Well, it must have some pretty stunning visuals to have garnered such a reaction because it sure as heck isn't the story..be barely capable of higher thought. However, she is unwittingly subjected to an experiment that causes her ascension (development of a higher consciousness) and she is then removed from the fast-food franchise and thrust into the middle of a burgeoning civil war.<br /><br />Now, I say interesting but that is only in relation to the other novellas. B008BDZOZY|5.0|David Byrne has always been an important musical thinker as far as I'm concerned anyway. Always very strongly attuned to rhythms he was one of the few people of his era able to see the possibilities in the African concept of polyrhythms in music and finding new ways to expand on that. He not only did that as a founding member of Talking Heads but also through a couple decades of creatively groundbreaking solo recordings. Now there are a number of artists taking up his league such as Annie Clarke,better known as St.Vincent,who is in her way seeking to expand on the perceived foundations of \\"pop\\" music. So the two combined in 2010 to begin recording this project that would not only combine her songwriting with his rhythmic sensibilities,but also add to that an enormous brass section arranged by people like Lenny Pickett but also electronic beats from John Congleton. With all these cooks in the kitchen,would it be possible for the duo's talents to be able to express their musical points coherently. The results are more surprising than one might think.<br /><br />  One can dress it up any way they want,but the foundation of the music is here is still very much on funk. You can here that on songs such as the opening songs \\"Who\\",\\"Weekend In The Dust\\",\\"Dinner For Two\\",\\"Ice Age\\" and \\"I Am An Ape\\". Not only are Byrne and Clarke's musical outlooks both highly compatible but on these five numbers the groove is laid on thick for some uniquely produced funk/pop epics. \\"The Forest Awakes\\" and \\"I Should Watch TV\\" showcase a more prominant Afro-latin rhythm. Nothing new for Byrne of course but the mixture of them with some of the orchestral horn arrangements and creative use of melody again creates a potent groove based set up. On the former song,in fact the influence of the Minneapolis/Prince style use of the drum machine,prominent in hip-hop in years to come,is firmly in play. The only real full on orchestral/progressive pop song on here is \\"Optimist\\" with Clarke on lead vocals. Towards the end of the album Byrne takes over heavy on the chunky,soulful groove of \\"Lightening\\" and the afro funk explosion of \\"The One Who Broke Your Heart\\" with the Dap Kings and the like minded afropop band Antibalas. The album ends with the slower,epic groove of \\"Outside Of Space And Time\\" with the swelling and romantic lyrical imagery suiting the music to a tee.<br /><br />  For many people who've considered the innovation of funk in dance music during the 70's as a passing fad usually indelibly linked with the still sometimes controversial disco era,Byrne has consistently proved critics and musical scholars wrong on that regard with his creative way with funk rhythms and his promotion of the original talents who produced them. Funny thing is,only on rare occasions has this ever attracted a wide audience. And for that mostly earlier on in his career. Since he left Talking Heads in the late 1980's,most innovations in pop music have been based in some variation on the dour end of the alternative rock scene. The funk end of it only seemed to crop up occasionally and even then pushed far into the backround with other things. Even if his solo work was hit or miss affairs commercially,he stuck to his guns that the 70's funk/soul era provided the pop world with an excellent base for expansion in it's future. By blending that aesthetic with the musical and songwriting talents of St. Vincent he was again able to fashion yet another successful hybrid from this. An excellent instrumental and melodic epic that should be enough for even the most jaded musical cynic to realize,in terms of music,it hasn't \\"all been done\\".Towards the end of the album Byrne takes over heavy on the chunky,soulful groove of \\"Lightening\\" and the afro funk explosion of \\"The One Who Broke Your Heart\\" with the Dap Kings and the like minded afropop band Antibalas. The album ends with the slower,epic groove of \\"Outside Of Space And Time\\" with the swelling and romantic lyrical imagery suiting the music to a tee.<br /><br />  For many people who've considered the innovation of funk in dance music during the 70's as a passing fad usually indelibly linked with the still sometimes controversial disco era,Byrne has consistently proved critics and musical scholars wrong on that regard with his creative way with funk rhythms and his promotion of the original talents who produced them. Funny thing is,only on rare occasions has this ever attracted a wide audience. And for that mostly earlier on in his career. Since he left Talking Heads in the late 1980's,most innovations in pop music have been based in some variation on the dour end of the alternative rock scene. Even if his solo work was hit or miss affairs commercially,he stuck to his guns that the 70's funk/soul era provided the pop world with an excellent base for expansion in it's future. By blending that aesthetic with the musical and songwriting talents of St. Vincent he was again able to fashion yet another successful hybrid from this. An excellent instrumental and melodic epic that should be enough for even the most jaded musical cynic to realize,in terms of music,it hasn't \\"all been done\\".
B003XF1XOQ|1.0|I keep having to remind myself that I am not the target audeince for these books. I suppose it's an excuse of sorts. Still I can't help but think that Ms. Collins took the easy way out by some of her choices.<br /><br />By choosing to write in the Young Adult genre, she ensures that her audience likely won't have much of a body of work to compare her books to. This is beneficial for her, otherwise her readers might recognize that her work is highly derivative of works like Shirley Jackson's 'The Lottery' and William Golding's 'Lord of the Flies' and even Stephen King's 'The Running Man' (writing as Richard Bachman).<br /><br />Her choice to confine the reader to the first person perspective of Katniss may help her readers identify with the protagonist, but it also severely limits the amount of world building she can do. All through this trilogy I wanted to know more about Panem. We get a brief history of the civil war that led to the Capitol's dominance which is just a device to explain how the Hunger Games started. We don't get much of a glimpse of life in the other districts. There's not even a good explanation of the geography of Panem. We don't know how big it is, where the individual districts are located, how far each one is from the Capitol, or what the climate is like. Almost nothing.<br /><br />Lastly, and there's no delicate way to put this, Katniss is annoying. Collins somehow manages the feat of making her completely self-absorbed and almost equally un-self-aware. This is where it would have helped Ms. Collins to have some other viewpoint characters.<br /><br />Some minor complaints:<br /><br />In book two, 'Catching Fire', Collins goes back to the well and - wait for it - puts Katniss back in the arena with Peeta. When I saw this was her intent, I couldn't believe it. I thought for sure Collins would come up with some device to avoid having to trod over this same ground again. But, no, there we are. The Quarter Quell aka Hunger Games Redux!<br /><br />Too much time is wasted in the beginning of the third book spent convincing Katniss to 'become the Mockingjay'. I suppose the idea of the leaders of the revolution being media savvy and essentially branding and marketing the revolt is mildly interesting. (Again, we are limited to Katniss' understanding of it.) Still the idea that anyone other than Katniss could be the Mockingjay is pretty dumb. Katniss was the one who defied the Capitol in the first Hunger Games by coming up with the idea of her and Peeta threatening to kill themselves. She was the one who popularized the mockingjay symbol by adopting it as her totem. She is the only recognizable figure symbolizing defiance through all the districts. Who else are they going to get? And yet there is much mental handwringing on Katniss' part over whether or not she will 'become the Mockingjay'.<br /><br />Another thing I noticed is that in true American neo-Puritan fashion there is a shocking amount of violence but no sex. Katniss and Peeta and Katniss and Gale do plenty of kissing but that's it. Yet there is no shortage of blood, including many children being killed. Surely in this world, the lifespans in the districts must be shortened by captivity, forced labor and near starvation conditions. That being the case I would expect children to mature to adulthood faster, with marriage and child-bearing coming earlier. There is no hint of this.<br /><br />Again, I will remind myself: I am not the target audience. Yet I can't help thinking what a nice dystopian sci-fi trilogy Ms. Collins might have had, had she been willing to expand her perspective a little bit and work a little more on the world building.oo much time is wasted in the beginning of the third book spent convincing Katniss to 'become the Mockingjay'. B003QP4NPE|3.0|There is a burgeoning segment of the science fiction genre that deals with gaming, augmented reality, social networking. Books in this genre that I have read recently include 'Reamde' by Neal Stephenson, 'Rule 34' by Charles Stross, and 'This Is Not A Game' by Walter Jon Williams.<br /><br />'Daemon' is a worthy addition to this genre. According to the dust jacket Suarez is an enterprise IT consultant to Fortune 1000 companies. As an IT professional myself, I can say that Mr. Suarez seems to know his stuff. I couldn't find any obvious mistakes.<br /><br />The 'Daemon' referred to in the title isn't a denizen of hell or another plane. Rather it's a piece of software that executes once certain conditions are met. I don't like to give too much away so I will say that the Daemon is a very complex piece of software that, once set in motion, threatens to upend the economic and political and social order. So, obviously the stakes are high.<br /><br />Suarez does have several instances where characters lecture us about the problems in modern society but it's nothing too original (haves and have not, evil corporations, fat cats). And while the Daemon is threatening this order, it's less clear what the Daemon's author imagines will replace it. I am hoping this will be revealed in subsequent books.<br /><br />The reader follows a group of about six to eight throughout the novel with a shifting perspective. I didn't really feel like we got to know any of the characters very well. That being said, the reader isn't going to be reading a book like this for the depth or complexity of the character portraits. Rather the reader will be reading it for Suarez' command of the technology and how he imagines it being put to use.<br /><br />Mostly his imaginings are a good combination of thought-provoking and all-to-real. He does go a bit off the rails towards the end (robot motorcycles with swords - and, no, I am not making that up).<br /><br />One thing about 'Daemon' is that it does leave you hanging at the end of the book so if you want to know what happens, my guess is that you probably will have no choice but to read books two and three of the trilogy. Money paid is considered  rent and the fine animal will be returned once he comes back from the war.<br /><br />A few weeks / months later, the boy receives the sketchbook of the officer and learns he was killed in action. To the now distraught boy, upset that Joey might have been killed as well console their son saying it was possible Joey was nowhere near the the officer. Therefore Joey may still be alive and perfectly fine.  Meaning the boy cared more about the horse than the dead officer.  In retrospect this is perhaps \\"natural,\\" it is a movie about a horse and after all, the boy knew the officer for all of 5 minutes while doting over the animal for several years.<br /><br />Plot holes exist for dynamic effect, the girl gallops up a hill and is in the middle of a horde of German solders.  Could she not hear or at worst, see the Germans as she poked her head above the rise then turn about before she was in the middle of them?  Perhaps in the real world, yes, but that does not make for swell movie dramatics.<br /><br />As the Germans retreated, the Joey finds himself trapped by a traveling British tank (possibly a Mark V).  The tank was alone, no other troops nearby, odd since such tanks usually have supporting infantry with them.  The horse reaches the a dead end blocked by wire and high steep embankments on all sides.  No way out for the horse.  No way out for the tank and its crew either, perfect spot for a German ambush, or get stuck.  WHERE in the HECK was that tank going?!? The film asks us to stretch our  limits of believability at times , where no one wants to put out of its misery, a wired up suffering horse in the middle of no-man's land. B003U74NQW|5.0|This movie is one of the greatest ever made.  It is mysterious, beautifully-filmed, emotional, and weird--very weird, and yet it's not \\"weird for weird's sake.\\"  The weirdness here is the perversion of the repressed libido (the movie starts with the main character's dad falling to the ground after his \\"water hose gets blocked.\\")<br /><br />There is so much commentary on this movie, it seems silly to delve into it here. What you can rest assured that this is a true cinematic experience, and even today (I just watched it again yesterday), it still has the power to push buttons and be controversial.  But really, underneath, it's just a really well-written movie, with layers of themes, symbolism, music, imagery--all working together to create a cinematic punch you won't find anywhere else.<br /><br />The main theme is that on the surface things might seem perfect and picturesque in any small American town, but underneath, in the dark, in the ground, are bugs and insects...evil people like Frank Booth.  Even the ending, which shows a bird eating a bug is deceptive. It's a facade like everything else.<br /><br />Then again, there are many angles to cut up this tasty pie of a movie.  For movie geeks, there is a ton to analyze here. 0142000671|5.0|John Steinbeck's Of Mice and Men is both an enlightening and heartbreaking story of two friends going through a life of work together. George is a small man who is committed to taking care of Lennie, a large, strong, mentally disabled man, after his Aunt Clara's death. George always complains about how his life would be easier if he didn't have to take care of Lennie, but truly cares for him and tells him of their plans to have a farm of their own when they get enough money. Lennie loves the story of their future farm and always asks George to tell him about how someday he will get to take care of the rabbits. Lennie loves the soft touch of animals' hair which later gets him into trouble with the wife of a man who works on the farm of their new job.<br /><br /> Lennie always seemed to be the outcast in the novel, even by George who told him not to speak when going for their new job, for fear that Lennie would not be allowed to work, or be seen as \\"dumb.\\" Published in 1937, Of Mice and Men does not show signs of inclusion for those who are mentally disabled, nor any understanding of those who have disabilities. However, even though this novel does not show good examples of inclusion or understanding, it can still teach its readers positively about individuals with disabilities.<br /><br /> Although this is a short novel, John Steinbeck allows this book to pull at its readers' hearts as well as teach lessons of life and love through the relationship of George and Lennie. Of Mice and Men would be a perfect book to teach at a high school level even though it is a seemingly easy read. There are themes throughout the book that would help students understand isolation, friendship, and strengths versus weaknesses. Even if there are no students with disabilities in the classroom or the school, students can learn from this novel the importance of inclusion even outside of the classroom. A class can even learn from this story about alienating students who don't have disabilities but still feel that they do not \\"fit in.\\" I would recommend this book to anyone who loves a good read, no matter what age. I think that it teaches its readers a lot about life, no matter who comes into our lives and no matter how different they may be.

0374214913|5.0|Overview:<br />When I selected this invigorating little novel, I saw the description which likened it to luminaries Murakami Haruki, Neal Stephenson, and Umberto Eco. With a panel of names like that, there really wasn't a choice NOT to pick it up. Not to mention a positive review from that Curator of the Curious, Nick Harkaway.<br /><br />A quick search using your favorite browser software and the title of this book should churn up the author's own site, where a short story version of this can be read for free. The book is a different story, though, so you'd probably be better off stopping after the description of the bookstore in the short, online version.<br /><br />A. Plot/Backstory<br />Clay needs a job. Recently let go as an industrial designer/artist, Clay needs to find a way to pay rent. On an introspective walk, he notices a strange little store, called Mr. Penumbra's 24-Hour Bookstore. Upon entering, he finds a curious, dusty, and book-lined crevasse whose owner greets him with a twinkle in his eye and the question, \\"What do you seek?\\"<br /><br />If that doesn't catch your attention, you probably should just walk away now. If it does, you probably don't need to read any more. If you're in the 1% of people who still can't make up your mind, the rest of the review should hopefully convince you.<br /><br />One of the most refreshing aspects of the plot here is that the author doesn't make this a battle of good vs. evil. The world isn't getting destroyed here, and, ultimately, the conflicts, the resolutions, and the rewards are things that are personal, not global. And that is kind of nice. It isn't a globe-trotting adventure of high-finance and international spies that we're talking about...it's a bunch of nerds and their books, their fonts, their designs. At the end of the day, it's not something that will get your adrenaline pumping, but it will probably put a smile on your face, which is really much better.<br /><br />B. Characters<br />There are several interesting characters here. The most important of these are Clay, Mr. Penumbra, and Kat (Clay's girlfriend). The supporting cast is also strange, fun, and generally unique.<br /><br />Clay is your typical everygeek. He used to work in website design, but takes the job at Mr. Penumbra's because he needs the money. But you get the feeling that Clay would probably have taken it over something like his old job anyway, since he is a curious and kindred spirit to Mr. Penumbra. Through the course of the story, its fun to watch Clay develop into a more complete person, which is something characters should always do.<br /><br />Kat, Clay's girlfriend, is an ideal girlfriend for most geeks. She's smart, beautiful, and nerdy. But she does tend to be somewhat absorbed in her own things in the course of the book. Not that this is inherently a bad thing, but it does tend to limit her involvement as a character. It also makes her seem a bit...obsessive and spooky. She doesn't really grow as much as Clay in the book, either. But the resolution of the Kay and Clay plotline was something that I enjoyed.<br /><br />Mr. Penumbra is probably one of the most curious characters in the book (at least...main characters; the supporting characters are just bizarre). Penumbra (and his counterpart) represent something of the same battle as the stated conflict between Old Knowledge and New Knowledge. The conflict between the two demonstrates most of the development of Penumbra's character. But he really feels kind of fun, like a grandpa that you wish that you had.<br /><br />C. Setting<br />The story is set in a wealth of different locations, from Mr. Penumbra's titular bookstore to the the underground vaults of the Unbroken Spine, from Clay's weird little apartment to the hallowed halls of Google. Nicely enough, each setting in interesting, described well, and they generally contribute to the plot.<br /><br />D. Themes<br />The major theme here is transcendance. Each of the characters are seeking to accomplish a goal, which is growing past some limitation that they have. Watching most of their quests and developments leave you feeling pretty good.<br /><br />There is a clear secondary theme of the conflict between progress and the associated uncertainty that it brings and conservativism and the stagnation that it represents. The author has a clear preference, as shown in the resolution of the conflict between the two sides, but the absence of vilification of either side (or calling either side unambigiously heroic) makes it really nice.<br /><br />E. Point of View/Voice<br />The story is told from the point of view of Clay, which allows us to discover the mysteries of the Unbroken Spine alongside him. The voice is third person, limited omniscience, which is just about perfect. Third person omniscient from a perspective watching Clay would also have been an interesting, if vastly harder to pull off, choice.<br /><br />F. Aesthetics<br />The book generally FEELS legitimately like its from Clay's perspective, which is the perspective that people who will enjoy this book will share (i.e., the perspective of a nerd). While it remains to be seen (with his second book), whether this was a conscious effort or merely Robin Sloan's writing style, it certainly works here. It contributes very nicely to the story.<br /><br />Conclusion:<br />I started recommending this to people before I reached page fifty. That's usually a good sign, although it's occasionally risky, because authors can sometimes screw it up. Especially first authors. Robin Sloan, who is a first author, carries it off well, and I will be eagerly anticipating his second book.<br /><br />A<br /><br />Harkius

0061537969|5.0|The Art of Racing in the Rain was recomended to me back in 2009. I was skeptical at first because I don't really read dramatic novels. I don't have anything against them, I'm just more of a fantasy/horror guy. But when I learned that the story was told from a dog's point of view I decided to give it a go being a huge dog person myself. I'm glad that I did.<br /><br />Enzo has quickly become one of my favorite characters of all time. He is a wise and perceptive dog who is ready to become a man in his next life. He hates not being able to talk, he watches too much TV and he loves his owner Denny. Denny is a race car driver and according to Enzo one of the best there is. The story actually begins when Denny meets Eve. They eventually have their daughter Zoe and everything seems perfect. But it doesn't last. Denny goes through many hardships throughout the story which include losing his wife to cancer and fighting for custody of his daughter.<br /><br />The author uses his own love and experiences of racing as metaphors for much of what Denny has to endure and overcome. And while the story is mainly about the love that Denny has for his wife and daughter it is also about the connection he has with Enzo. They both share an almost mystical and spiritiual bond that drives (no pun intended whatsoever) the story wonderfully.<br /><br />The story is pretty heartbreaking, there's no doubt about that but it will also make you laugh (beware the evil zebra) and maybe even make you think about the way you look at dogs. I would recommend it whether you're a dog person or not or whether you're a racing fan or not because it is about so much more than that. It's about fighting for what you love and about not giving up even when things seem hopeless. I have read this book a few times since coming across it and I'm certain that I will revisit Enzo many more times in the future, like revisiting an old friend who I haven't seen in a while, only this friend happens to walk on all fours.

But when Red Mist and his gang kill one of the the new heroes in a gross and disturbing way, then kill a number of toddlers , civilians and police officers while engaging in a rape of one Kick Ass's acquaintances, this all seems to be so far fetched as to be ridiculous. Supposedly, Red Mist gathered the info about Kick Ass's crush during the torture scene in Kick Ass 1. You remember that , when they had a car battery attached to his genitals and were torturing him for info as to who hired Big Daddy and Hit Girl? What's the likelihood that Red Mist would care or even remember the details of Dave's crush? Why would he? For all he knew, he as certain Kick Ass was going to die after they got the information about who hired Big Daddy. That was the only relevant information. Anything else was of very little value unless it pertained to that. It could not be used against him later as he thought KickAss would be dead later. No, this plot point was added so that, like in every bad revenge piece, the girl can be raped by the villain.  It was formulaic and pointless. I just started asking the simple questions: If villains are being instructed to commit mass murder just for fun mind you) via the internet, wouldn't the police be aware? If Dave's friend at school knows the identity of one of the Goth guys in his class as one of the villains on the board, wouldn't the cops be aware? If Red Mist was calling for a super villain terror plot, and Chris Genovese was found in a Red Mist costume surrounded by the 34 bodies of his father and dad's goons about a year prior, shouldn't the cops be looking for him? Shouldn't the mob be out stopping him? Would Goth kids who want to be villains for kicks and giggles really keep up when this idiot starts lopping peoples heads off? Would even hardened criminals with any sense of self preservation not understand that killing cops and little kids has no upside and won't get you very rich at the end of the day?<br /><br />Even with the rape, even when the kids died, even when a primary character died,  I really felt little as by that time I was chalking the whole of Kick Ass 2 as a wet dream Red Mist is having in Bellevue's loony bin. The carnage is so cartoonish, the reactions so fake , it boggles the mind. This is definitely, in Kick Ass' terms, a \\"Heroes Season 2\\" book. I hope in Kick Ass 3, the first scene is his dad coming out of shower, and Kick Ass amazed that it must have all been a bad dream.

0525952926|5.0|I'd like to begin by saying why I was able to read and write a review of a 925 page book on the day of its release. I preordered this book a couple of months ago, when the release date was Sep 11th. I was sent the book, received it on Sep 12th and spent a few days reading it. When I went to Amazon to post my review, I found the release date had been moved back to Sep 18th and that I couldn't post my review. So, here it is now! I'm rather curious how many other readers also received their book a week early?<br /><br />Anyway, my review...<br />Ken Follett's new novel, \\"Winter of the World\\", is the second in the planned three volume set about the history of the 20th century. Beginning in 1933, Follett brings his huge cast of characters along from the years up to the end of the Great War. To talk about the plot of the new book is impossible. Way too many characters and too many plot points. BUT, Follett's such a good writer that he brings the reader up to date with ALL his characters. Follett gives most of his characters enough nuance that few seem like caricatures.<br /><br />The interesting thing about Follett's second book is the breadth of the coverage of the 1930's and 40's (and into the `50's).  Everything from the burning of the Reichstag to the T4 Euthanesia program under the Nazis, to the bombing of Pearl Harbor and the battle of Midway to the development of the atomic bomb is covered. Now, in a regular novel, the reader would think, \\"oh yeah, how can one character or family of characters be present at all these historic events?\\" But Follett has developed so many characters that what happens is not unlikely. His characters seem to merge with each other and then separate much like the designs in a kaleidoscope. The American heiress from the Russian-emigree father goes to England in the mid-1930's and marries the son(s) of members the British/Welsh nobility. The German characters interact with both the British and the Russians. All these families had been introduced in Follett's first book and all interacted in Follett's second.<br /><br />Something else interesting I noticed from Follett's first book and his second is the fact that none of the major characters in the first book died. They had to survive to make the second book possible. Now in the second book, several of the main characters do die, which, given the war setting, is a bit more believable. However, many children are also born by book's end and these children will star in the third book in the Follett trilogy.<br /><br />Also, and this is important. Follett doesn't do a lot of reintroducing characters, their relationships, and plot points from the first book to the second. I guess he just assumes most readers have read the first book and so know the characters of the second. As a result, there's little awkwardness to his writing and the second book flows pretty naturally.<br /><br />A question a new reader might ask is if he should read the first book,\\"Fall of the Giants\\" before \\"Winter of the World\\"? This second book could be a stand-alone novel. Follett sets an ambitious course with his proposed three volume set. So far, with the first and second books, he's done quite well.<br /><br />I don't normally write such short reviews but there's no way to talk about the plot except to say Follett is a master. And if you don't like the book, you can always use it as a door stop. It is a large volume, containing a great story. Enjoy.uced in Follett's first book and all interacted in Follett's second.<br /><br />Something else interesting I noticed from Follett's first book and his second is the fact that none of the major characters in the first book died. They had to survive to make the second book possible. Now in the second book, several of the main characters do die, which, given the war setting, is a bit more believable. However, many children are also born by book's end and these children will star in the third book in the Follett trilogy.<br /><br />Also, and this is important. Follett doesn't do a lot of reintroducing characters, their relationships, and plot points from the first book to the second.
